        <title>Tandem gene duplications drive divergent evolution of caffeine and crocin biosynthetic pathways in plants | BMC Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Plants have evolved a panoply of specialized metabolites that increase their environmental fitness. Two examples are caffeine, a purine psychotropic alkaloid, and crocins, a group of glycosylated apocarotenoid pigments. Both classes of compounds are found in a handful of distantly related plant genera (Coffea, Camellia, Paullinia, and Ilex for caffeine; Crocus, Buddleja, and Gardenia for crocins) wherein they presumably evolved through convergent evolution. The closely related Coffea and Gardenia genera belong to the Rubiaceae family and synthesize, respectively, caffeine and crocins in their fruits. Here, we report a chromosomal-level genome assembly of Gardenia jasminoides, a crocin-producing species, obtained using Oxford Nanopore sequencing and Hi-C technology. Through genomic and functional assays, we completely deciphered for the first time in any plant the dedicated pathway of crocin biosynthesis. Through comparative analyses with Coffea canephora and other eudicot genomes, we show that Coffea caffeine synthases and the first dedicated gene in the Gardenia crocin pathway, GjCCD4a, evolved through recent tandem gene duplications in the two different genera, respectively. In contrast, genes encoding later steps of the Gardenia crocin pathway, ALDH and UGT, evolved through more ancient gene duplications and were presumably recruited into the crocin biosynthetic pathway only after the evolution of the GjCCD4a gene. This study shows duplication-based divergent evolution within the coffee family (Rubiaceae) of two characteristic secondary metabolic pathways, caffeine and crocin biosynthesis, from a common ancestor that possessed neither complete pathway. These findings provide significant insights on the role of tandem duplications in the evolution of plant specialized metabolism."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcbiol.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Tandem gene duplications drive divergent evolution of caffeine and crocin biosynthetic pathways in plants"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Plants have evolved a panoply of specialized metabolites that increase their environmental fitness. Two examples are caffeine, a purine psychotropic alkaloid, and crocins, a group of glycosylated apocarotenoid pigments. Both classes of compounds are found in a handful of distantly related plant genera (Coffea, Camellia, Paullinia, and Ilex for caffeine; Crocus, Buddleja, and Gardenia for crocins) wherein they presumably evolved through convergent evolution. The closely related Coffea and Gardenia genera belong to the Rubiaceae family and synthesize, respectively, caffeine and crocins in their fruits. Here, we report a chromosomal-level genome assembly of Gardenia jasminoides, a crocin-producing species, obtained using Oxford Nanopore sequencing and Hi-C technology. Through genomic and functional assays, we completely deciphered for the first time in any plant the dedicated pathway of crocin biosynthesis. Through comparative analyses with Coffea canephora and other eudicot genomes, we show that Coffea caffeine synthases and the first dedicated gene in the Gardenia crocin pathway, GjCCD4a, evolved through recent tandem gene duplications in the two different genera, respectively. In contrast, genes encoding later steps of the Gardenia crocin pathway, ALDH and UGT, evolved through more ancient gene duplications and were presumably recruited into the crocin biosynthetic pathway only after the evolution of the GjCCD4a gene. This study shows duplication-based divergent evolution within the coffee family (Rubiaceae) of two characteristic secondary metabolic pathways, caffeine and crocin biosynthesis, from a common ancestor that possessed neither complete pathway. These findings provide significant insights on the role of tandem duplications in the evolution of plant specialized metabolism."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12915.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Plants have evolved a panoply of specialized metabolites that increase their environmental fitness. Two examples are caffeine, a purine psychotropic alkaloid, and crocins, a group of glycosylated apocarotenoid pigments. Both classes of compounds are found in a handful of distantly related plant genera (<i>Coffea</i>, <i>Camellia</i>, <i>Paullinia</i>, and <i>Ilex</i> for caffeine; <i>Crocus</i>, <i>Buddleja</i>, and <i>Gardenia</i> for crocins) wherein they presumably evolved through convergent evolution. The closely related <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Gardenia</i> genera belong to the Rubiaceae family and synthesize, respectively, caffeine and crocins in their fruits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here, we report a chromosomal-level genome assembly of <i>Gardenia jasminoides</i>, a crocin-producing species, obtained using Oxford Nanopore sequencing and Hi-C technology. Through genomic and functional assays, we completely deciphered for the first time in any plant the dedicated pathway of crocin biosynthesis. Through comparative analyses with <i>Coffea canephora</i> and other eudicot genomes, we show that <i>Coffea</i> caffeine synthases and the first dedicated gene in the <i>Gardenia</i> crocin pathway, <i>GjCCD4a</i>, evolved through recent tandem gene duplications in the two different genera, respectively. In contrast, genes encoding later steps of the <i>Gardenia</i> crocin pathway, ALDH and UGT, evolved through more ancient gene duplications and were presumably recruited into the crocin biosynthetic pathway only after the evolution of the <i>GjCCD4a</i> gene.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>This study shows duplication-based divergent evolution within the coffee family (Rubiaceae) of two characteristic secondary metabolic pathways, caffeine and crocin biosynthesis, from a common ancestor that possessed neither complete pathway. These findings provide significant insights on the role of tandem duplications in the evolution of plant specialized metabolism.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Flowering plants have evolved a diverse array of secondary metabolites to repel pathogens and predators, attract pollinators, and drive ecosystem functions. In many cases, the genomic context for the evolution of specialized plant compounds involves tightly linked clusters of genes, usually containing nonhomologous gene families, that together control novel biosynthetic pathways [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nutzmann HW, Huang A, Osbourn A. Plant metabolic clusters - from genetics to genomics. New Phytol. 2016;211:771–89." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d45765e903">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chae L, Kim T, Nilo-Poyanco R, Rhee SY. Genomic signatures of specialized metabolism in plants. Science. 2014;344:510–3." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d45765e903_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Guo L, Winzer T, Yang X, Li Y, Ning Z, He Z, Teodor R, Lu Y, Bowser TA, Graham IA, Ye K. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science. 2018;362:343–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d45765e906">3</a>]. A few important metabolic clusters involve only tandem duplicates within single gene families, such as the <i>N</i>-methyltransferase (<i>NMT</i>) genes that control caffeine biosynthesis in the coffee plant [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud F, Carretero-Paulet L, Dereeper A, Droc G, Guyot R, Pietrella M, Zheng C, Alberti A, Anthony F, Aprea G, et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science. 2014;345:1181–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d45765e915">4</a>], and the cytochrome p450 genes encoding the 2,4-dihydroxy-7-methoxy-1,4-benzoxazin-3-one (DIMBOA) metabolic cluster of maize, which produces an important defense compound [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Frey M, Chomet P, Glawischnig E, Stettner C, Grun S, Winklmair A, Eisenreich W, Bacher A, Meeley RB, Briggs SP, et al. Analysis of a chemical plant defense mechanism in grasses. Science. 1997;277:696–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d45765e919">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dutartre L, Hilliou F, Feyereisen R. Phylogenomics of the benzoxazinoid biosynthetic pathway of Poaceae: gene duplications and origin of the Bx cluster. BMC Evol Biol. 2012;12:64." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d45765e922">6</a>]. Tandem gene duplicate clusters originally arise as copy number variants (CNVs) in populations that later become fixed within species by evolving split or novel functions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Salojarvi J, Smolander OP, Nieminen K, Rajaraman S, Safronov O, Safdari P, Lamminmaki A, Immanen J, Lan T, Tanskanen J, et al. Genome sequencing and population genomic analyses provide insights into the adaptive landscape of silver birch. Nat Genet. 2017;49:904–12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d45765e925">7</a>]. Given the ongoing nature of CNV production during evolution, genome sequencing of closely related plants harboring distinct secondary metabolite profiles holds great promise for understanding the stepwise evolution of important tandem duplicate clusters.</p><p>The <i>Gardenia</i> genus, which is among the most commonly grown horticultural plants worldwide and is valued for the strong, sweet fragrance of its flowers, belongs to the family Rubiaceae. In this large family of angiosperms, only the <i>Coffea canephora</i> (robusta coffee) genome has been sequenced to date [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud F, Carretero-Paulet L, Dereeper A, Droc G, Guyot R, Pietrella M, Zheng C, Alberti A, Anthony F, Aprea G, et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science. 2014;345:1181–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d45765e937">4</a>]. The Chinese species <i>Gardenia jasminoides</i> (gardenia) has been cultivated for at least 1000 years and was introduced to Europe and America in the mid-eighteenth century. The fruits of <i>G. jasminoides</i>, whose major bioactive constituents are genipin and crocins, were used as an imperial dye for royal costumes during the Qin and Han dynasties in China and are recorded in the Chinese Pharmacopoeia [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Xiao W, Li S, Wang S, Ho CT. Chemistry and bioactivity of Gardenia jasminoides. J Food Drug Anal. 2017;25:43–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d45765e947">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chinese Pharmacopoeia Commission. Pharmacopoeia of the People’s Republic of China. Beijing: China Medical Science and Technology Press; 2015." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d45765e950">9</a>]. Unlike coffee, gardenia does not accumulate caffeine. However, in a pattern similar to the scattered instances of convergent caffeine biosynthesis among several angiosperm families [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud F, Carretero-Paulet L, Dereeper A, Droc G, Guyot R, Pietrella M, Zheng C, Alberti A, Anthony F, Aprea G, et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science. 2014;345:1181–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d45765e953">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Huang R, O'Donnell AJ, Barboline JJ, Barkman TJ. Convergent evolution of caffeine in plants by co-option of exapted ancestral enzymes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:10613–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d45765e956">10</a>], crocins are found in the flowers of the distantly related plants <i>Buddleja davidii</i> (Buddlejaceae) and in the stigmas of <i>Crocus sativus</i> (saffron) (Iridaceae) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig1">1</a>a).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="298" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Crocin biosynthesis. <b>a</b> Simplified angiosperm phylogenetic tree. Crocin-synthesizing genera are shown in red (<i>Gardenia</i>), green (<i>Buddleja</i>), and purple (<i>Crocus</i>), and caffeine-synthesizing genera (<i>Coffea</i>) in brown. The red dot marks the divergence of <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Gardenia</i>. The divergence time between <i>G. jasminoides</i> and <i>C. canephora</i> was estimated at approximately 20.69 MYA. <b>b</b> Crocin accumulation in different <i>G. jasminoides</i> organs. R, root; St, stem; L, leaf; Fl, flower; Frl, fruitlet; GF, green fruit; RF, red fruit; Sa, sarcocarp</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Apocarotenoids, derived from carotenoids by oxidative cleavage [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma G, Zhang L, Matsuta A, Matsutani K, Yamawaki K, Yahata M, Wahyudi A, Motohashi R, Kato M. Enzymatic formation of beta-citraurin from beta-cryptoxanthin and Zeaxanthin by carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase4 in the flavedo of citrus fruit. Plant Physiol. 2013;163:682–95." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1024">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang B, Liu C, Wang Y, Yao X, Wang F, Wu J, King GJ, Liu K. Disruption of a CAROTENOID CLEAVAGE DIOXYGENASE 4 gene converts flower colour from white to yellow in Brassica species. New Phytol. 2015;206:1513–26." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1024_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ohmiya A, Kishimoto S, Aida R, Yoshioka S, Sumitomo K. Carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase (CmCCD4a) contributes to white color formation in chrysanthemum petals. Plant Physiol. 2006;142:1193–201." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1027">13</a>], play crucial roles in plants as signaling molecules, flower and fruit pigments, and regulators of membrane fluidity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hou X, Rivers J, Leon P, McQuinn RP, Pogson BJ. Synthesis and function of apocarotenoid signals in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 2016;21:792–803." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1030">14</a>]. They have been reported to have anticancer, anti-inflammatory, antioxidant, and anti-diabetic activities and to be beneficial in the treatment of central nervous system and cardiovascular diseases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Khorasanchi Z, Shafiee M, Kermanshahi F, Khazaei M, Ryzhikov M, Parizadeh MR, Kermanshahi B, Ferns GA, Avan A, Hassanian SM. Crocus sativus a natural food coloring and flavoring has potent anti-tumor properties. Phytomedicine. 2018;43:21–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1033">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Nair SC, Pannikar B, Panikkar KR. Antitumour activity of saffron (Crocus sativus). Cancer Lett. 1991;57:109–14." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1036">16</a>]. Crocin biosynthesis in saffron stigmas is initiated by carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase 2 (CsCCD2), which cleaves zeaxanthin to produce crocetin dialdehyde [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Frusciante S, Diretto G, Bruno M, Ferrante P, Pietrella M, Prado-Cabrero A, Rubio-Moraga A, Beyer P, Gomez-Gomez L, Al-Babili S, Giuliano G. Novel carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase catalyzes the first dedicated step in saffron crocin biosynthesis. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2014;111:12246–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1040">17</a>]. The aldehyde dehydrogenase CsALDH3I1 and the UDP-glucosyltransferase CsUGT74AD1 perform, respectively, the dehydrogenation of crocetin dialdehyde to crocetin and its glycosylation to crocins 1 and 2′ [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Demurtas OC, Frusciante S, Ferrante P, Diretto G, Azad NH, Pietrella M, Aprea G, Taddei AR, Romano E, Mi J, et al. Candidate enzymes for saffron crocin biosynthesis are localized in multiple cellular compartments. Plant Physiol. 2018;177:990–1006." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1043">18</a>]. The UGT mediating the formation of more highly glycosylated crocins is still uncharacterized [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Demurtas OC, Frusciante S, Ferrante P, Diretto G, Azad NH, Pietrella M, Aprea G, Taddei AR, Romano E, Mi J, et al. Candidate enzymes for saffron crocin biosynthesis are localized in multiple cellular compartments. Plant Physiol. 2018;177:990–1006." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1046">18</a>]. In <i>Buddleja</i> flowers, only the zeaxanthin cleavage step has been characterized, and it is mediated by BdCCD4.1 and BdCCD4.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ahrazem O, Diretto G, Argandona J, Rubio-Moraga A, Julve JM, Orzaez D, Granell A, Gomez-Gomez L. Evolutionarily distinct carotenoid cleavage dioxygenases are responsible for crocetin production in Buddleja davidii. J Exp Bot. 2017;68:4663–77." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1052">19</a>]. Thus, it appears that crocin biosynthesis in eudicots (<i>Buddleja</i>) and monocots (<i>Crocus</i>) has evolved through the convergent evolution of different CCD subclasses (CCD2 and CCD4, respectively) that have acquired the capacity to cleave zeaxanthin at the at the 7/8,7′/8′ positions to produce crocetin dialdehyde.</p><p>In <i>G. jasminoides</i>, crocins are accumulated in green and red fruits (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig1">1</a>b). The <i>Gardenia</i> crocin biosynthesis pathway has not yet been elucidated, in spite of the availability of transcriptome data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ji A, Jia J, Xu Z, Li Y, Bi W, Ren F, He C, Liu J, Hu K, Song J. Transcriptome-guided mining of genes involved in crocin biosynthesis. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:518." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1074">20</a>]. Two <i>G. jasminoides</i> UGTs, GjUGT94E5 and GjUGT75L6, are able to catalyze the two-step conversion of crocetin into crocins in vitro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nagatoshi M, Terasaka K, Owaki M, Sota M, Inukai T, Nagatsu A, Mizukami H. UGT75L6 and UGT94E5 mediate sequential glucosylation of crocetin to crocin in Gardenia jasminoides. FEBS Lett. 2012;586:1055–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1081">21</a>]. However, the expression profiles of the corresponding genes are not consistent with their proposed role in crocin biosynthesis in <i>G. jasminoides</i> fruits [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ji A, Jia J, Xu Z, Li Y, Bi W, Ren F, He C, Liu J, Hu K, Song J. Transcriptome-guided mining of genes involved in crocin biosynthesis. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:518." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1087">20</a>]. Additionally, the lack of genomic information for crocin-producing species hampers the elucidation of the mechanisms underlying the molecular evolution of crocin biosynthesis. Recently, sequencing of the <i>C. canephora</i> (Rubiaceae) and <i>Camellia sinensis</i> (Theaceae) genomes has shown that the synthesis of caffeine, a purine alkaloid, has evolved independently in the two genera through tandem duplication and neofunctionalization of different <i>N</i>-methyltransferase (NMT) ancestral genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud F, Carretero-Paulet L, Dereeper A, Droc G, Guyot R, Pietrella M, Zheng C, Alberti A, Anthony F, Aprea G, et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science. 2014;345:1181–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1100">4</a>].</p><p>Here, we report a chromosome-level assembly of the highly heterozygous <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome, using a combination of Illumina short reads, Oxford Nanopore (ONT) long reads, and Hi-C scaffolding. The genes involved in the crocin biosynthesis pathway were identified through functional assays, and the molecular evolution of crocin and caffeine biosynthesis in the Rubiaceae family was clarified through comparative genomic studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Chromosome-level assembly of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM1">1</a>)</h3><p>The size of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome was predicted to be 550.6 ± 9 Mb (± SD) based on flow cytometry and 547.5 Mb based on 17 <i>k</i>-mer distribution analysis and to show a very high level (about 2.2%) of heterozygosity (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S1). A highly collapsed and fragmented genome was assembled by ALLPATHS-LG using Illumina shotgun reads (293× coverage) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S1). This assembly was 635.6 Mb in size (28% of N bases) and was composed of 58,859 scaffolds (N50, 60.6 kb) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3).</p><p>To improve the contiguity of the assembly, we generated 2,675,530 Oxford Nanopore (ONT) long reads with an N50 of 21.6 kb. The longest reads were 366.8 kb, and the genome coverage was about 60× (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S2). After testing different de novo assembly pipelines, we identified a package (Canu-SMARTdenovo-3×Pilon) that yielded satisfactory results (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3). A contiguous assembly of 677.9 Mb with a contig N50 of 703.1 kb was produced, and the longest contig in the assembly was 11.7 Mb. Since the assembly size was larger than predicted genome size, we ran Purge Haplotigs to collapse the highly heterozygous regions. The purged assembly was 534.1 Mb long with a contig N50 of 1.0 Mb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3). The completeness of the genome assembly was assessed with the BUSCO pipeline, which found 95.0% complete BUSCOs, of which 92.7% were single-copy and 2.3% duplicated (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3).</p><p>The contiguity of the assembly was further improved using Hi-C scaffolding. There were 56,933,122 paired-end reads representing valid interactions were used to scaffold 99.5% of the assembly into 11 chromosomes using the Lachesis package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Belton JM, McCord RP, Gibcus JH, Naumova N, Zhan Y, Dekker J. Hi-C. a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods. 2012;58:268–76." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1162">22</a>] (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig2">2</a>a). The final corrected chromosome-level genome was 535 Mb in size, 531 of which were assembled in the 11 chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Tab1">1</a>). The <i>G. jasminoides</i> chromosomes showed a significant level of synteny with their <i>C. canephora</i> counterparts, with a limited number of translocations between the two genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig2">2</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S2). In addition, a 154,919-bp chloroplast genome and 640,334-bp mitochondrial genome of <i>G. jasminoides</i> were assembled and identified.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="407" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome. <b>a</b> Chromosome-level assembly of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome using Hi-C technology. <b>b</b> Synteny between <i>C. canephora</i> and <i>G. jasminoides</i> chromosomes. The positions of <i>NMT</i> genes, catalyzing caffeine biosynthesis in <i>Coffea</i>, and of <i>CCD</i>, <i>ALDH</i>, and <i>UGT</i> genes, catalyzing crocin biosynthesis in <i>Gardenia</i>, are marked</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Metrics of the final assembly and annotation of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>The <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome comprises 35,967 protein-coding genes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Tab1">1</a>). Consistent with the assessment of genome assembly quality, orthologs of 96.5% of eukaryotic BUSCOs were identified in the <i>G. jasminoides</i> gene sets (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Tab1">1</a>). Transposable elements (TEs) account for approximately 54.0% (288,723,343 nt) of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S4, 5, 6), and 62.2% of these TEs are long terminal repeat (LTR) elements. We identified 1798 full-length LTR elements including 709 Gypsy and 403 Copia elements. These elements have an average insertion time of 1.4 million years ago (MYA) assuming a mutation rate of <i>μ</i> = 1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup> (per bp per year) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xu Z, Xin T, Bartels D, Li Y, Gu W, Yao H, Liu S, Yu H, Pu X, Zhou J, et al. Genome analysis of the ancient tracheophyte Selaginella tamariscina reveals evolutionary features relevant to the acquisition of desiccation tolerance. Mol Plant. 2018;11:983–94." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1452">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="VanBuren R, Wai CM, Ou S, Pardo J, Bryant D, Jiang N, Mockler TC, Edger P, Michael TP. Extreme haplotype variation in the desiccation-tolerant clubmoss Selaginella lepidophylla. Nat Commun. 2018;9:13." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1455">24</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S3), and <i>Gypsy</i> elements are much younger than <i>Copia</i> elements (1.2 MYA vs 1.7 MYA, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05). Most 5s rRNAs are tandemly arrayed in Chr 11 of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome, and the tandem repeats of 18s rRNAs (average size of 1.8 kb), 5.8s rRNA (average size of 154 bp), and 28s rRNAs (average size of 6.3 kb) were clustered in Gardenia 10, spanning 65.9 kb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S7, 8).</p><p>34,662 orthologous gene groups were found for <i>G. jasminoides</i> and 10 additional angiosperms, covering 335,254 genes in total. Among these, 155,220 genes clustering into 6671 groups were conserved in all plants examined, and 1248 gene families containing 5123 genes appeared to be unique to <i>G. jasminoides</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S4A). Eight hundred ninety-one gene families were expanded, and 2666 gene families were contracted in the <i>G. jasminoides</i> lineage (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S4A). <i>G. jasminoides</i>-specific and expanded gene families comprised glycoside hydrolases (PF00232) and UDP-glucosyl/glucuronosyl transferases (PF00201), which might be related to the metabolism of bioactive compounds such as crocins and geniposides (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S9). Based on phylogenomic analysis of 121 single-copy genes, the divergence time between <i>G. jasminoides</i> and <i>C. canephora</i> was estimated at approximately 20.7 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig1">1</a>a, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S4B).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Identification of <i>G. jasminoides</i> candidate crocin biosynthetic genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM3">3</a>)</h3><p>The mature fruit of <i>G. jasminoides</i> exhibits a visible reddish yellow or brown color, due to the presence of crocins, crocetin, and geniposides [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nagatoshi M, Terasaka K, Owaki M, Sota M, Inukai T, Nagatsu A, Mizukami H. UGT75L6 and UGT94E5 mediate sequential glucosylation of crocetin to crocin in Gardenia jasminoides. FEBS Lett. 2012;586:1055–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1532">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Gao L, Zhu BY. The accumulation of crocin and geniposide and transcripts of phytoene synthase during maturation of Gardenia jasminoides fruit. Evid Based Complement Alternat Med. 2013;2013:686351." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1535">25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen Y, Zhang H, Li YX, Cai L, Huang J, Zhao C, Jia L, Buchanan R, Yang T, Jiang LJ. Crocin and geniposide profiles and radical scavenging activity of gardenia fruits (Gardenia jasminoides Ellis) from different cultivars and at the various stages of maturation. Fitoterapia. 2010;81:269–73." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1538">26</a>], which are found in the mature pericarp and sarcocarp of the fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig1">1</a>b). We collected samples from three different fruit developmental stages: fruitlet with a green pericarp and immature sarcocarp, green fruit with a green pericarp and red sarcocarp, and red fruit with red pericarp and red sarcocarp. Analysis of different organs and tissues, including the root, stem, leaf, flower, fruitlet, green fruit, and red fruit, indicated that crocins are specifically localized in green and red fruits, whose sarcocarps have matured (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig1">1</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S5-S6). Transcriptome reads from seven <i>G. jasminoides</i> organs were mapped to the assembled genome and annotated gene loci to calculate gene expression (fragments per kilobase of exon per million reads mapped, FPKM). Genome-wide analysis of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> assembly identified fourteen <i>CCD</i> genes that might be involved in the tailoring of carotenoids for apocarotenoid formation (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S7, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S10). The <i>GjCCD4</i> and <i>GjCCD8</i> gene subfamilies were expanded in the <i>G. jasminoides</i> lineage (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S10). <i>GjCCD4a</i> was highly expressed in green and red fruits (FPKM &gt; 1000), in accordance with the distribution of crocins, while <i>GjCCD4a</i>, <i>GjCCD4c</i>, and <i>GjCCD4d</i> were highly expressed in flowers (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S10, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S11). GjCCD4a (ARU08109.1) showed high amino acid sequence similarity to GjCCD4b (81%), GjCCD4c (81%), and GjCCD4d (77%), and the four genes were located on a single gene cluster on chr 9 (see below).</p><p>Eighteen <i>G. jasminoides</i> genes with similarity to aldehyde dehydrogenases (ALDHs), which catalyze the oxidation of aldehydes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Brocker C, Vasiliou M, Carpenter S, Carpenter C, Zhang Y, Wang X, Kotchoni SO, Wood AJ, Kirch HH, Kopecny D, et al. Aldehyde dehydrogenase (ALDH) superfamily in plants: gene nomenclature and comparative genomics. Planta. 2013;237:189–210." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1604">27</a>], were identified and classified into 10 distinct subfamilies (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S8, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S12). In <i>C. sativus</i>, CsALDH3I1 is known to mediate with high efficiency the dehydrogenation of crocetin dialdehyde to crocetin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Demurtas OC, Frusciante S, Ferrante P, Diretto G, Azad NH, Pietrella M, Aprea G, Taddei AR, Romano E, Mi J, et al. Candidate enzymes for saffron crocin biosynthesis are localized in multiple cellular compartments. Plant Physiol. 2018;177:990–1006." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1617">18</a>]; however, expression of <i>GjALDH3</i> genes was low in the fruit and thus inconsistent with the crocin distribution in <i>G. jasminoides</i> organs. Instead, <i>GjALDH2C3</i> (KY631926.1) was highly expressed in green and red fruits and flowers (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S10, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S12).</p><p>Two hundred thirty-seven <i>UGT</i> genes were identified in the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome and were classified into 19 subfamilies (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S9, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S13). More than 50% are members of the <i>UGT79</i>, <i>UGT73</i>, <i>UGT85</i>, and <i>UGT94</i> groups. Eleven <i>UGT</i> genes were significantly expressed in green and red fruits (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">10</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S13).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Elucidation of the <i>G. jasminoides</i> crocin biosynthetic pathway</h3><p>We used expression in <i>E. coli</i> to test the activity of the candidate crocin biosynthetic genes identified by expression analysis. A strain co-transformed with pET32a-<i>CCD4a</i> and the zeaxanthin accumulation plasmid pACCAR25ΔcrtX showed discoloration, and a new product with a retention time and characteristic fragment ions ([M + H]<sup>+</sup>: <i>m/z</i> 297.1939) identical to that of the crocetin dialdehyde standard was observed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>a, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S11).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="304" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Elucidation of the crocin biosynthesis pathway in <i>G. jasminoides.</i><b>a</b> UPLC-DAD chromatograms (abs at 440 nm) of <i>E. coli</i> extracts expressing GjCCD4a. <b>b</b>–<b>d</b> UPLC-DAD chromatograms (abs at 440 nm) of in vitro reactions catalyzed by GjALDH2C3 (<b>b</b>), GjUGT74F8 (<b>c</b>), and GjUGT94E13 (<b>d</b>). St, standards; EV, empty vector; lyc, lycopene; β-car, β-carotene; zea, zeaxanthin; cro, crocetin; CD, crocetin dialdehyde; CS, crocetin semialdehyde; CrI–V, crocins I–V</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In both <i>Crocus</i> and <i>Buddleja</i>, the substrate for crocetin dialdehyde formation is zeaxanthin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Frusciante S, Diretto G, Bruno M, Ferrante P, Pietrella M, Prado-Cabrero A, Rubio-Moraga A, Beyer P, Gomez-Gomez L, Al-Babili S, Giuliano G. Novel carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase catalyzes the first dedicated step in saffron crocin biosynthesis. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2014;111:12246–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1753">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ahrazem O, Diretto G, Argandona J, Rubio-Moraga A, Julve JM, Orzaez D, Granell A, Gomez-Gomez L. Evolutionarily distinct carotenoid cleavage dioxygenases are responsible for crocetin production in Buddleja davidii. J Exp Bot. 2017;68:4663–77." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1756">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ahrazem O, Rubio-Moraga A, Berman J, Capell T, Christou P, Zhu C, Gomez-Gomez L. The carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase CCD2 catalysing the synthesis of crocetin in spring crocuses and saffron is a plastidial enzyme. New Phytol. 2016;209:650–63." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1759">28</a>] (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig1">1</a>b). Since lycopene and β-carotene are also possible substrates, we expressed GjCCD4a in <i>E. coli</i> strains accumulating these carotenoids. In both cases, we detected the formation of crocetin dialdehyde (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>a) and, in the case of β-carotene, also of the intermediate product 8′-apo-β-carotenal (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>a, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S12, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S14). In contrast, none of the other <i>Gardenia</i> or <i>Coffea</i> CCD4 proteins was able to cleave zeaxanthin or β-carotene (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S13). Protein structure modeling and docking analysis of GjCCD4a suggested the capacity to bind all three substrates, in accordance with its catalytic activity (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S14).</p><p>Next, we tested the ability of the purified GjALDH2C3 protein to catalyze in vitro the oxidation of crocetin dialdehyde. Incubation of crocetin dialdehyde with GjALDH2C3 yielded two new peaks, and the retention time (7.84 min) and characteristic fragment ions ([M + H]<sup>+</sup>: <i>m/z</i> 327.1605) of the most abundant peak were consistent with those of the crocetin standard. In addition, the intermediate product crocetin semialdehyde (8.68 min and [M + H]<sup>+</sup>: <i>m/z</i> 311.1686) was also detected (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S15).</p><p>As noted above, a large number of <i>UGT</i> genes are expressed in <i>Gardenia</i> fruits, and these are likely involved in the synthesis of several classes of glycosylated compounds in these organs. The enzymatic activities of twelve candidate UGTs were characterized in vitro using crocetin as a substrate (marked with an asterisk in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S13). Three UGTs, namely GjUGT74F8, GjUGT75L6, and GjUGT94E13, were able to glycosylate crocetin and/or crocins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>c, d, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S16-S21). Of these, only two (GjUGT74F8 and GjUGT94E13) were expressed at high levels in fruits, suggesting that they are involved in crocin production, while the third, described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nagatoshi M, Terasaka K, Owaki M, Sota M, Inukai T, Nagatsu A, Mizukami H. UGT75L6 and UGT94E5 mediate sequential glucosylation of crocetin to crocin in Gardenia jasminoides. FEBS Lett. 2012;586:1055–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1833">21</a>], was not significantly expressed in these organs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S10).</p><p>GjUGT74F8, which is the most highly expressed among all UGTs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S10, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S13), is similar to CsUGT74AD1, which is responsible for crocin primary glycosylation in <i>C. sativus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Demurtas OC, Frusciante S, Ferrante P, Diretto G, Azad NH, Pietrella M, Aprea G, Taddei AR, Romano E, Mi J, et al. Candidate enzymes for saffron crocin biosynthesis are localized in multiple cellular compartments. Plant Physiol. 2018;177:990–1006." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1852">18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Moraga AR, Nohales PF, Perez JA, Gomez-Gomez L. Glucosylation of the saffron apocarotenoid crocetin by a glucosyltransferase isolated from Crocus sativus stigmas. Planta. 2004;219:955–66." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d45765e1855">29</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S22). Upon in vitro incubation of GjUGT74F8 with crocetin, two new peaks with retention times of 4.96 and 6.30 min were detected. The newly formed products had the same fragmentation patterns as crocin III ([M + Na]<sup>+</sup>, 675.2620) and crocin V ([M + Na]<sup>+</sup>, 513.2090), respectively, demonstrating that GjUGT74F8 has the ability to add one or two β-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-glucoses to the carboxyl groups of crocetin (primary glycosylation) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>c, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S20). In addition, reversible conversion of crocin II to crocin IV and vice versa was observed, suggesting that GjUGT74F8 possesses also a hydrolytic activity, able to remove a single β-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-glucose (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>c, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S20).</p><p>In vitro incubation of crocetin with purified GjUGT94E13 resulted in the generation of two new products with retention times of 4.22 and 5.75 min, respectively. These two compounds had mass spectra with [M + Na]<sup>+</sup> peaks at <i>m/z</i> 999.3699 and 675.2610, respectively, consistent with those of crocin I and crocin IV (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>d, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S16). We also found that crocin IV was gradually converted to crocin I by the extension of the incubation time with GjUGT94E13 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>d, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S16) and that GjUGT94E13 could efficiently catalyze the glycosylation of crocins II and III to crocin I (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig3">3</a>d, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S17, S18). In no case was generation of a crocin with a single β-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-glucosyl ester detected under different reaction conditions, suggesting that GjUGT94E13 could catalyze either the addition of a second glucosyl group to a pre-existing one (secondary glycosylation) or the sequential addition of two glucosyl groups to a carboxyl group (primary and secondary glycosylation).</p><p>Based on these results, the complete crocin biosynthesis pathway in <i>G. jasminoides</i> is depicted in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig4">4</a>. Lycopene, β-carotene, and zeaxanthin are cleaved at the 7/8,7′/8′ positions by GjCCD4a, yielding crocetin dialdehyde, which is then converted to crocetin by GjADH2C3. Crocetin is the substrate of two UGTs, GJUGT74F8 and GjUGT94E13, adding respectively one or two glucose esters to the two carboxylic groups.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/4"
