src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="570" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The crocin biosynthesis pathway in <i>G. jasminoides</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evolution of crocin and caffeine biosynthesis genes in the Rubiaceae</h3><p>Synteny analysis demonstrated clearly that <i>Coffea</i> caffeine synthases and <i>Gardenia CCD4a</i> are localized, respectively, in tandemly repeated arrays that are unique to each of the two species, suggesting the two gene duplication series occurred after the <i>Coffea</i>-<i>Gardenia</i> divergence (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig5">5</a>a, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S23-24). The <i>Coffea</i> caffeine synthase gene cluster is syntenic to non-caffeine-producing NMTs in the <i>Gardenia</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig5">5</a>a, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S23), while the close Gentianales relative <i>Gelsemium</i> (Gelsemiaceae) does not even contain NMTs in the homologous chromosomal regions. The latter observation suggests that the common ancestral genomic block in all gentianalean species lacked an <i>NMT</i> gene and that the ancestor of the future caffeine synthase cluster translocated there during Rubiaceae evolution, where it duplicated in <i>Coffea</i> only after the divergence of <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Gardenia</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig5">5</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S24). Conversely, the <i>Gardenia</i> gene cluster comprising <i>GjCCD4a-b-c-d</i> is syntenic to a unique <i>CCD4</i> gene in the coffee genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig5">5</a>a, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S15), yielding an inverted evolutionary scenario wherein local duplications in <i>Gardenia</i> that occurred after <i>Coffea</i>-<i>Gardenia</i> common ancestry led to the production of distinct bioactive compounds. In contrast, the downstream crocin biosynthesis genes <i>GjALDH2C3</i>, <i>GjUGT74F8</i>, and <i>GjUGT94E5</i> are localized in gene clusters that appear conserved between <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Gardenia</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S24), suggesting that they may have been generated before the split of the two genera.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00795-3/MediaObjects/12915_2020_795_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="729" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><i>Coffea</i> caffeine synthases and <i>Gardenia</i> crocin biosynthesis dioxygenase arose through genus-specific tandem duplications. <b>a</b> Microsynteny between <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Gardenia</i> around the caffeine synthases of the former and the first dedicated gene in crocin biosynthesis in <i>Gardenia</i>, <i>GjCCD4a</i>. <b>b</b> Simplified phylogenies (pruned from the trees provided in Figs. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM4">27</a>-S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM5">28</a>) depicting the evolution of caffeine and crocin biosynthetic genes in Gentianales. Purple branches indicate clades that are Gentianales-specific; green, Rubiaceae-specific; red, <i>Coffea</i>-specific. The gene IDs used for the phylogenetic trees of NMTs and CCD are the following: CcNMT2 (Cc02_g09350), CcNMT4b (Cc09_g07000), CcNMT4a (Cc09_g06990), CcMTL (Cc09_g06950), CcNMT3 (Cc09_g06960), CcXMT (Cc09_g06970), CcDXMT (Cc01_g00720), CcMXMT (Cc00_g24720), GjNMT2 (Gj9A1032T108), GjNMT4a (Gj1A458T26), GjNMT4b (Gj1X458T25), GjNMT4c (Gj1A458T28), SlCCD4a (Solyc08g075480.2.1), SlCCDb (Solyc08g075490.2.1), CcCCD4 (Cc08_g05610), GjCCD4a (Gj9A597T69), GjCCD4b (Gj9A597T68), GjCCD4c (Gj9A597T67), GjCCD4d (Gj9P597T6), GsCCD4 (Gs_scaff312_1.38), CrCCD4a (CRO_T140281), CrCCD4b (CRO_T140282), CrCCD4c (CRO_T140277), and CgCCD4 (cal_g006885.t1). Gene IDs for those with the biochemical activities in question are shown in red. <i>Gardenia</i> gene IDs are shown in orange. All other gene IDs are shown in black. For the collapsed tree branches in the pruned trees, species abbreviations are as follows: At, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>; Vv, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>; Sl, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>; Gs, <i>Gelsemium sempervirens</i>; Cr, <i>Catharanthus roseus</i>; Cg, <i>Calotropis gigantea</i>; Cc, <i>Coffea canephora</i>; Gj, <i>Gardenia jasminoides</i>; Bd, <i>Buddleja davidii</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Phylogenetic analyses of NMTs related to caffeine synthases and CCDs related to crocin synthase confirmed this evidence based on synteny. Caffeine biosynthetic enzymes belong to a lineage of NMTs that are specific to Rubiaceae, however, ones that duplicated and specifically evolved caffeine biosynthetic function only in <i>Coffea</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig5">5</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S26, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM4">4</a>: Fig. S27). Similarly, the CCD4 subfamily wherein <i>Gardenia</i>’s crocin synthase resides is Rubiaceae-specific, but not tandemly duplicated in <i>Coffea</i> as it is in <i>Gardenia</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#Fig5">5</a>b, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM5">5</a>: Fig. S28). The <i>UGT74</i> and <i>UGT94</i> gene subfamilies in both Rubiaceae species are contained within distinct subclades in a large UGT family tree, each grouping with related genes from other Gentianales in sublineages likely to be of ancient origin (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM6">6</a>: Fig. S29). Similarly, the ALDH2 genes of <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Gardenia</i> are close relatives in a subclade also comprising genes from other Gentianales species (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM7">7</a>: Fig. S30).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Chromosome-level assembly of a highly heterozygous genome</h3><p>Many plants including medicinal ones such as <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xu H, Song J, Luo H, Zhang Y, Li Q, Zhu Y, Xu J, Li Y, Song C, Wang B, et al. Analysis of the genome sequence of the medicinal plant Salvia miltiorrhiza. Mol Plant. 2016;9:949–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2199">30</a>], <i>Panax ginseng</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Xu J, Chu Y, Liao B, Xiao S, Yin Q, Bai R, Su H, Dong L, Li X, Qian J, et al. Panax ginseng genome examination for ginsenoside biosynthesis. Gigascience. 2017;6:1–15." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2205">31</a>], <i>Panax notoginseng</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Chen W, Kui L, Zhang G, Zhu S, Zhang J, Wang X, Yang M, Huang H, Liu Y, Wang Y, et al. Whole-genome sequencing and analysis of the Chinese herbal plant Panax notoginseng. Mol Plant. 2017;10:899–902." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2212">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhang D, Li W, Xia EH, Zhang QJ, Liu Y, Zhang Y, Tong Y, Zhao Y, Niu YC, Xu JH, Gao LZ. The medicinal herb Panax notoginseng genome provides insights into ginsenoside biosynthesis and genome evolution. Mol Plant. 2017;10:903–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2215">33</a>], and <i>Glycyrrhiza uralensis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Mochida K, Sakurai T, Seki H, Yoshida T, Takahagi K, Sawai S, Uchiyama H, Muranaka T, Saito K. Draft genome assembly and annotation of Glycyrrhiza uralensis, a medicinal legume. Plant J. 2017;89:181–94." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2221">34</a>] are highly heterozygous, resulting in fragmented genome assemblies when using traditional short read methods. <i>G. jasminoides</i>, the second plant of Rubiaceae with a sequenced genome, is self-incompatible with high (2.2%) heterozygosity. We developed an efficient method to produce chromosome-level assemblies for heterozygous plant genomes using a combination of short Illumina and long ONT reads and Hi-C scaffolding [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Belton JM, McCord RP, Gibcus JH, Naumova N, Zhan Y, Dekker J. Hi-C. a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods. 2012;58:268–76." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2227">22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Schmidt MH, Vogel A, Denton AK, Istace B, Wormit A, van de Geest H, Bolger ME, Alseekh S, Mass J, Pfaff C, et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell. 2017;29:2336–48." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2231">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Burton JN, Adey A, Patwardhan RP, Qiu R, Kitzman JO, Shendure J. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat Biotechnol. 2013;31:1119–25." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2234">36</a>].  The assemblies constructed using short reads were extremely fragmented, while the combination of short and ONT reads with a Canu-SMARTdenovo assembly pipeline produced a <i>G. jasminoides</i> assembly with the highest contiguity. The significant difference in the number of annotated repeat sequences between the ONT (53.97%) and Illumina (36.51%)-based assemblies indicates the importance of genome contiguity for annotation of repetitive elements (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3). However, the ONT-based assembly size was much larger than the genome size predicted by <i>k</i>-mer distribution and flow cytometry, suggesting that highly divergent haplotypes were assembled separately. This was confirmed and corrected by haplotig purging, whereafter the assembly was scaffolded into chromosomal pseudomolecules using Hi-C. Given the low cost of ONT long reads, the pipeline described here provides an extremely cost-effective method for producing chromosome-level assemblies of highly heterozygous plant genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Characterization of the <i>Gardenia</i> crocin biosynthetic pathway</h3><p>In a previous transcriptomic study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ji A, Jia J, Xu Z, Li Y, Bi W, Ren F, He C, Liu J, Hu K, Song J. Transcriptome-guided mining of genes involved in crocin biosynthesis. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:518." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2258">20</a>], we identified several <i>G. jasminoides</i> unigenes bearing high sequence similarity to candidate genes for crocin biosynthesis (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S16). However, the low number of tissues analyzed by RNA-Seq (3 versus 7 in the present study) and the high level of fragmentation of the unigenes prevented the unambiguous identification of bona fide candidates for all the steps through co-expression analysis, as well as the expression in <i>E. coli</i> of the corresponding full-length proteins for functional assays. Based on genome-wide candidate gene identification, expression studies, and in vitro functional studies, three of the 7 previously identified unigenes (<i>GjCCD4a</i>, <i>GjALDH2C3</i>, and <i>UGT94E13</i>), but also a novel <i>UGT74F8</i> gene, which went undetected in the previous transcriptome analysis, were shown to be involved in crocin biosynthesis in <i>G. jasminoides</i>.</p><p>In <i>C. sativus</i>, zeaxanthin is cleaved symmetrically at the 7/8,7′/8′ positions by CsCCD2 to produce crocetin dialdehyde [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Frusciante S, Diretto G, Bruno M, Ferrante P, Pietrella M, Prado-Cabrero A, Rubio-Moraga A, Beyer P, Gomez-Gomez L, Al-Babili S, Giuliano G. Novel carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase catalyzes the first dedicated step in saffron crocin biosynthesis. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2014;111:12246–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2292">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ahrazem O, Rubio-Moraga A, Berman J, Capell T, Christou P, Zhu C, Gomez-Gomez L. The carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase CCD2 catalysing the synthesis of crocetin in spring crocuses and saffron is a plastidial enzyme. New Phytol. 2016;209:650–63." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2295">28</a>], while, in <i>B. davidii</i>, the same reaction is carried out by BdCCD4.1 and BdCCD4.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ahrazem O, Diretto G, Argandona J, Rubio-Moraga A, Julve JM, Orzaez D, Granell A, Gomez-Gomez L. Evolutionarily distinct carotenoid cleavage dioxygenases are responsible for crocetin production in Buddleja davidii. J Exp Bot. 2017;68:4663–77." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2301">19</a>]; no activity of CsCCD2 and/or BdCCD4.1/BdCCD4.3 on other carotenoids, including β-carotene and lycopene, has been reported. GjCCD4a from <i>G. jasminoides</i> shares only 31% identity with CsCCD2, but 56 and 59% identity with BdCCD4.1 and BdCCD4.3, respectively, and can catalyze the symmetric 7/8,7′/8′ cleavage of zeaxanthin, β-carotene, and lycopene to produce crocetin dialdehyde. Thus, crocetin biosynthesis evolved in different plant taxa through convergent evolution, since the carotenoid cleavage step is catalyzed by a CCD2 in <i>Crocus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Frusciante S, Diretto G, Bruno M, Ferrante P, Pietrella M, Prado-Cabrero A, Rubio-Moraga A, Beyer P, Gomez-Gomez L, Al-Babili S, Giuliano G. Novel carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase catalyzes the first dedicated step in saffron crocin biosynthesis. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2014;111:12246–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2311">17</a>], but by CCD4 enzymes in <i>Buddleja</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ahrazem O, Diretto G, Argandona J, Rubio-Moraga A, Julve JM, Orzaez D, Granell A, Gomez-Gomez L. Evolutionarily distinct carotenoid cleavage dioxygenases are responsible for crocetin production in Buddleja davidii. J Exp Bot. 2017;68:4663–77." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2317">19</a>] and <i>Gardenia</i> (this paper). Convergent evolution also underlies the second step of crocin biosynthesis, which is mediated by an ALDH3 in <i>C. sativus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Demurtas OC, Frusciante S, Ferrante P, Diretto G, Azad NH, Pietrella M, Aprea G, Taddei AR, Romano E, Mi J, et al. Candidate enzymes for saffron crocin biosynthesis are localized in multiple cellular compartments. Plant Physiol. 2018;177:990–1006." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2327">18</a>] and by an ALDH2 in <i>G. jasminoides</i> (this paper).</p><p>Two <i>Gardenia</i> UGTs, GjUGT75L6 and GjUGT94E5, were previously shown to catalyze the conversion of crocetin to crocins in vitro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nagatoshi M, Terasaka K, Owaki M, Sota M, Inukai T, Nagatsu A, Mizukami H. UGT75L6 and UGT94E5 mediate sequential glucosylation of crocetin to crocin in Gardenia jasminoides. FEBS Lett. 2012;586:1055–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2339">21</a>]. However, their low expression in fruits suggests that they are not the primary enzymes involved in crocin biosynthesis in vivo. We identified two novel <i>UGT</i> genes, <i>GjUGT74F8</i> and <i>GjUGT94E13</i>, closely related to <i>GjUGT75L6</i> and <i>GjUGT94E5</i> that are highly expressed in fruits and show co-expression with <i>GjCCD4a</i>. <i>GjUGT74F8</i> was the most highly expressed <i>UGT</i> gene in mature fruit, and the protein product catalyzed the primary glycosylation of crocetin, the same reaction catalyzed by GjUGT75L6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nagatoshi M, Terasaka K, Owaki M, Sota M, Inukai T, Nagatsu A, Mizukami H. UGT75L6 and UGT94E5 mediate sequential glucosylation of crocetin to crocin in Gardenia jasminoides. FEBS Lett. 2012;586:1055–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2367">21</a>] and by <i>Crocus</i> UGT74AD1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Demurtas OC, Frusciante S, Ferrante P, Diretto G, Azad NH, Pietrella M, Aprea G, Taddei AR, Romano E, Mi J, et al. Candidate enzymes for saffron crocin biosynthesis are localized in multiple cellular compartments. Plant Physiol. 2018;177:990–1006." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2374">18</a>]. Similar to the latter, GjUGT74F8 did not exhibit a secondary glycosylation activity. GjUGT74F8 was also found to catalyze de-glycosylation reactions. Reversible glycosylation has been previously described in other plant UGTs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang X. Structure, mechanism and engineering of plant natural product glycosyltransferases. FEBS Lett. 2009;583:3303–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2377">37</a>]. GjUGT94E13 catalyzed primary and secondary glycosylation of crocetin to form crocins IV and I, presumably via the sequential addition of two β-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-glucosyl esters. Crocins V and II were undetectable when crocetin was incubated with GjUGT94E13, suggesting that the secondary glycosylation step occurred much faster than the primary glycosylation one. Incubation of crocin II or crocin III with GjUGT94E13 resulted in the complete conversion to crocin I, confirming that GjUGT94E13 is able to add a second glucosyl moiety to a mono-glycosyl group (secondary glycosylation).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Molecular evolution of caffeine and crocin biosynthesis in the Rubiaceae</h3><p>The chromosome-level <i>Gardenia</i> genome assembly reported here and its comparative analysis with the closely related <i>Coffea</i> genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Denoeud F, Carretero-Paulet L, Dereeper A, Droc G, Guyot R, Pietrella M, Zheng C, Alberti A, Anthony F, Aprea G, et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science. 2014;345:1181–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2397">4</a>] allowed a detailed reconstruction of the molecular events that led to the evolution of the caffeine and crocin pathways in the two genera. The <i>NMT</i> gene cluster involved in caffeine biosynthesis in <i>C. canephora</i> shows synteny to a region in <i>G. jasminoides</i> that also contains NMTs, but ones that predate the evolution of the caffeine synthase cluster characteristic of coffee. Conversely, the first dedicated gene in crocin biosynthesis, <i>GjCCD4a</i>, is part of a 4-gene cluster for which, in coffee, there is only one ortholog (<i>Cc08g05610</i>), showing the highest identity (80.8%) to <i>GjCCD4c</i>. The two genes are highly expressed in flowers in the two species, suggesting that they may be responsible for carotenoid cleavage in the white flowers of <i>G. jasminoides</i> and <i>C. canephora</i>, followed by volatile apocarotenoid formation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Brandi F, Bar E, Mourgues F, Horvath G, Turcsi E, Giuliano G, Liverani A, Tartarini S, Lewinsohn E, Rosati C. Study of ‘Redhaven’ peach and its white-fleshed mutant suggests a key role of CCD4 carotenoid dioxygenase in carotenoid and norisoprenoid volatile metabolism. BMC Plant Biol. 2011;11:24." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2426">38</a>]. Additionally, neither the CcCCD4 nor the GjCCD4b-c proteins exhibit 7/8,7′/8′ cleavage activity against any carotenoid tested. These findings suggest that caffeine and crocetin biosynthesis in coffee and <i>Gardenia</i> evolved, respectively, through tandem duplications and functional specialization of <i>NMT</i> and <i>CCD4</i> genes, and that these tandem duplications occurred after the separation of the two genera. Besides giving rise to novel metabolic functions, tandem gene duplications play several additional roles in genome evolution; for instance, in the birch and avocado genomes, tandem duplicates are enriched for pathogen responses [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Salojarvi J, Smolander OP, Nieminen K, Rajaraman S, Safronov O, Safdari P, Lamminmaki A, Immanen J, Lan T, Tanskanen J, et al. Genome sequencing and population genomic analyses provide insights into the adaptive landscape of silver birch. Nat Genet. 2017;49:904–12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2438">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Rendon-Anaya M, Ibarra-Laclette E, Mendez-Bravo A, Lan T, Zheng C, Carretero-Paulet L, Perez-Torres CA, Chacon-Lopez A, Hernandez-Guzman G, Chang TH, et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2019;116:17081–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2441">39</a>], while in the Australian carnivorous pitcher plant, tandem duplicates are enriched in enzymatic functions related to the acquisition of carnivory [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Fukushima K, Fang X, Alvarez-Ponce D, Cai H, Carretero-Paulet L, Chen C, Chang TH, Farr KM, Fujita T, Hiwatashi Y, et al. Genome of the pitcher plant Cephalotus reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory. Nat Ecol Evol. 2017;1:59." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2445">40</a>]. The production of tandem duplicates as copy number variants in populations, and their subsequent species-level fixation, provides a general evolutionary substrate for novel secondary metabolic activities in the recent adaptive landscapes of plant genomes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>This study sequenced the genome of <i>G. jasminoides</i>, a crocin-producing species, and dissected the complete crocin biosynthetic pathway through genomic and functional assays. Comparative analyses with <i>C. canephora</i> revealed that the caffeine biosynthetic genes (NMTs) in <i>Coffea</i> and the first dedicated crocin biosynthetic gene (GjCCD4a) in <i>Gardenia</i> evolved through recent tandem gene duplications in the two different genera, respectively. This study highlighted the divergent evolution of caffeine and crocin biosynthesis within the coffee family, providing significant insights on the role of tandem duplications in the evolution of plant specialized metabolism.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Plant materials</h3><p>An individual <i>G. jasminoides</i> (line 1–9) plant, which was asexually propagated by cutting, was obtained from Nanchuan District (29° N and 107° E), Chongqing City, China. Seven independent organs from <i>G. jasminoides</i>, including the root, stem, leaf, flower, fruitlet, green fruit, and red fruit, were collected. The fruitlet, green fruit, and red fruit represented different maturity. In total, 21 samples including three biological replicates for each organ were gathered. All samples were divided into two portions, which were used for the measurement of crocin content and RNA sequencing. The pooled young leaves were used to DNA extraction for Illumina and ONT sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">ONT sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Following the methods for megabase-size DNA preparation, we extracted the high molecular weight (HMW) genomic DNA of <i>G. jasminoides</i>, which was used to construct paired-end, mate-pair, and ONT libraries. The HMW gDNA was randomly fragmented using a Megaruptor; then, the large fragments were selected and purified using BluePippin and AMPure beads. After end-prep, ligation of sequencing adapters, and tether attachment, the fragments were sequenced on the ONT GridION X5 platform with 6 nanopore flow cells (v9.4.1). Base calling was performed using the Oxford Nanopore base caller Guppy (v1.8.5). Canu (v1.7) was used to correct, trim, and assemble the ONT raw reads with the default parameters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Koren S, Walenz BP, Berlin K, Miller JR, Bergman NH, Phillippy AM. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 2017;27:722–36." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2498">41</a>]. The correction-free approach, named Minimap/miniasm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Li H. Minimap and miniasm. Fast mapping and de novo assembly for noisy long sequences. Bioinformatics. 2016;32:2103–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2501">42</a>], was also independently performed with the recommended parameters. The assembler SMARTdenovo was also used for assembly with the corrected and trimmed ONT reads as input [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Istace B, Friedrich A, d'Agata L, Faye S, Payen E, Beluche O, Caradec C, Davidas S, Cruaud C, Liti G, et al. De novo assembly and population genomic survey of natural yeast isolates with the Oxford Nanopore MinION sequencer. Gigascience. 2017;6:1–13." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2504">43</a>]. The Canu-SMARTdenovo contigs were polished three times with Pilon (v1.22) using Illumina short reads [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Walker BJ, Abeel T, Shea T, Priest M, Abouelliel A, Sakthikumar S, Cuomo CA, Zeng Q, Wortman J, Young SK, Earl AM. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One. 2014;9:e112963." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2507">44</a>]. The final scaffolds were constructed with the polished contigs and corrected ONT reads using SSPACE-LongRead (version 1.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2511">45</a>], and heterozygous sequences were removed using Purge Haplotigs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Roach MJ, Schmidt SA, Borneman AR. Purge Haplotigs: allelic contig reassignment for third-gen diploid genome assemblies. BMC Bioinformatics. 2018;19:460." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2514">46</a>]. The quality of the genome assembly was estimated by searching for Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO v4.0, embryophyte profile) with Embryophya <i>odb</i> 10 dataset [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Simao FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:3210–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2520">47</a>]. Illumina sequences from the <i>G. jasminoides</i> DNA and RNA libraries were mapped to evaluate the quality of the assembled genome using BWA (Burrows-Wheeler Aligner) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:1754–60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2526">48</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Chromosome construction using Hi-C</h3><p>Fresh tissue of <i>G. jasminoides</i> was used to construct a Hi-C sequencing library. Steps included chromatin crosslinking, chromatin digestion with Hind III, biotin labeling and end repair, DNA purification, streptavidin pull-down of labeled Hi-C ligation products, and construction of an Illumina sequencing library. The clean sequences were mapped to the draft genome, and valid Hi-C reads were used to correct the draft assembly. Then, the draft genome of <i>G. jasminoides</i> was assembled into chromosomes (2n = 22) using Lachesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Belton JM, McCord RP, Gibcus JH, Naumova N, Zhan Y, Dekker J. Hi-C. a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods. 2012;58:268–76." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2543">22</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome annotation and RNA-Seq analysis</h3><p>Annotation of structural repeats in the <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome was performed using the RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/;">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/;</a> v1.0.9) package, which combines RECON and RepeatScout to identify and classify the repeat elements. The long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) in <i>G. jasminoides</i> were identified using LTR_Finder (v1.0.6) and LTR_retriever with the default parameters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ou S, Jiang N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 2018;176:1410–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2567">49</a>]. The repeat sequences were masked by RepeatMasker (v4.0.6) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>).</p><p>RNA-Seq on the HiSeq 4000 platform was performed for 21 samples. The short reads were assembled de novo using Trinity (v 2.2.0) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q, et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29:644–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2580">50</a>], and peptide sequences were predicted with TransDecoder (v2.1.0) (<a href="https://github.com/TransDecoder">https://github.com/TransDecoder</a>). The masked <i>G. jasminoides</i> genome annotation was ab initio predicted using the MAKER (v2.31.9) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SM, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, Holt C, Sanchez Alvarado A, Yandell M. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 2008;18:188–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2593">51</a>] annotation pipeline, integrating the assembled transcripts of <i>G. jasminoides</i> and protein sequences from <i>G. jasminoides</i>, <i>C. canephora</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i>. Noncoding RNAs were annotated by aligning to the Rfam database using INFERNAL (v1.1.2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Nawrocki EP, Eddy SR. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics. 2013;29:2933–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2609">52</a>], and miRNAs were further analyzed by performing BLASTN searches against the miRNA database. The RNA-Seq reads from different <i>G. jasminoides</i> organs were aligned to the masked genome using HiSAT2 (v2.0.5), and the FPKM values of annotated genes in the reference genome were calculated using Cufflinks (v2.2.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Trapnell C, Roberts A, Goff L, Pertea G, Kim D, Kelley DR, Pimentel H, Salzberg SL, Rinn JL, Pachter L. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat Protoc. 2012;7:562–78." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2615">53</a>].</p><p>The amino acid sequences of proteins from <i>G. jasminoides</i> and nine other angiosperms were clustered into orthologous groups using OrthoMCL (version 2.0.9) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ Jr, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2624">54</a>]. Phylogenetic analyses of single-copy orthologous genes were performed using the RAxML package (version 8.1.13) using the JTT+G+I substitution model for amino acid sequences with 1000 bootstrap replicates [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Stamatakis A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1312–3." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2627">55</a>]. Divergence times were directly estimated based on the divergence times of <i>P. trichocarpa</i>-<i>G. max</i> (94–127 MYA) and <i>B. distachyon</i>-<i>Z. mays</i> (40–53 MYA) obtained from TimeTree (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>). The Markov Cluster Algorithm (MCL) was used to identify species-specific gene groups [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Enright AJ, Van Dongen S, Ouzounis CA. An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. 2002;30:1575–84." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2650">56</a>]. CAFÉ (version 3.1) was used to predict gene family expansion and contraction [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="De Bie T, Cristianini N, Demuth JP, Hahn MW. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics. 2006;22:1269–71." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2653">57</a>]. Genome synteny analyses were performed using the CoGe web suite, <a href="http://www.genomevolution.org">www.genomevolution.org</a>, according to methods described elsewhere [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lyons E, Pedersen B, Kane J, Alam M, Ming R, Tang H, Wang X, Bowers J, Paterson A, Lisch D, Freeling M. Finding and comparing syntenic regions among Arabidopsis and the outgroups papaya, poplar, and grape: CoGe with rosids. Plant Physiol. 2008;148:1772–81." href="#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2664">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lyons E, Pedersen B, Kane J, Freeling M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the rosids. Trop Plant Biol. 2008;1:181–90." href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2664_1">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Ibarra-Laclette E, Lyons E, Hernandez-Guzman G, Perez-Torres CA, Carretero-Paulet L, Chang TH, Lan T, Welch AJ, Juarez MJ, Simpson J, et al. Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature. 2013;498:94–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2667">60</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Identification of gene families related to crocin biosynthesis</h3><p>Protein sequences of the CCD, ALDH, and UGT family members in <i>A. thaliana</i> were downloaded from the TAIR database, then were used as queries in BLASTP searches against the <i>G. jasminoides</i> protein sequences to identify homologous sequences. Full-length protein sequences were corrected and aligned with ClustalW2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Larkin MA, Blackshields G, Brown NP, Chenna R, McGettigan PA, McWilliam H, Valentin F, Wallace IM, Wilm A, Lopez R, et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:2947–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2685">61</a>]. Phylogenetic trees were constructed using the maximum likelihood method with the Jones-Taylor-Thornton (JTT) model and 1000 Bootstrap replicates [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Kumar S, Stecher G, Li M, Knyaz C, Tamura K. MEGA X: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis across computing platforms. Mol Biol Evol. 2018;35:1547–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2688">62</a>]. Further analyses incorporated blast searches (using <i>Gardenia</i> proteins as queries) of a number of other genomes to identify more CCD, ALDH, and UGT genes. For NMTs, the <i>Coffea canephora</i> XMT protein was used as a query (NCBI accession ABD90685.1). Species considered were <i>Gardenia jasminoides</i> (CoGe genome ID 53980), <i>Coffea canephora</i> (CoGe genome ID 19443), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (CoGe genome ID 16911), <i>Calotropis gigantea</i> (CoGe genome ID 36623), <i>Catharanthus roseus</i> (CoGe genome ID 36703), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (CoGe genome ID 19990), <i>Gelsemium sempervirens</i> (CoGe genome ID 53941), and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (CoGe genome ID 12289). Gene model IDs from the respective CoGe-uploaded genomes were retained as leaf IDs for phylogenetic analysis, with the exception that “:” when it appeared in a gene model ID was replaced by “_”. Several additional anchoring protein sequences from NCBI were incorporated in the NMT tree (MTL,_AFV60456.1; DXMT,_ABD90686.1; MXMT,_AFV60445.1; XMT,_ABD90685.1). Searches were run on the CoGe platform using default parameters and saving 100 Blast HSPs per species. Unique translated sequences were then downloaded, duplicates were excluded using BBedit, sequences with internal stop codons were excluded, and then trees were run using PASTA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Mirarab S, Nguyen N, Guo S, Wang LS, Kim J, Warnow T. PASTA: ultra-large multiple sequence alignment for nucleotide and amino-acid sequences. J Comput Biol. 2015;22:377–86." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2723">63</a>] with MAFFT [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Katoh K, Misawa K, Kuma K, Miyata T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 2002;30:3059–66." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2726">64</a>] to align the protein sequences and FastTree [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Price MN, Dehal PS, Arkin AP. FastTree 2--approximately maximum-likelihood trees for large alignments. PLoS One. 2010;5:e9490." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d45765e2729">65</a>] to create an approximately maximum likelihood tree. Trees were visualized and edited using FigTree (<a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/">http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/</a>) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM4">4</a>: Fig. S27, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM5">5</a>: Fig. S28, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM6">6</a>: Fig. S29, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM7">7</a>: Fig. S30). To interpret the supplemental figures, pink branches represent gentianalean clades, green branches represent Rubiaceae clades, and orange gene model IDs represent <i>Gardenia</i> genes. Coffee-specific clades are shown in red. In the NMT supplemental tree (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM4">4</a>: Fig. S27), the anchoring protein sequences are shown in red.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Enzymatic activity assays and LC/LC-MS analyses</h3><p>The cDNAs of candidate genes from the <i>CCD</i>, <i>ALDH</i>, and <i>UGT</i> families were de novo synthesized and cloned into expression vectors via digestion and ligation (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S25). The GjALDH2C3, GjUGT94E13, and GjUGT74F8 proteins were purified from <i>E. coli</i> using affinity chromatography to a purity &gt; 95% (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S15, S16, S19). The <i>in bacterio</i> and in vitro activity assays and detailed reaction mixtures are described in the Supplemental information. Crocetin dialdehyde (trans-crocetin dialdehyde) and crocetin (trans-crocetin) were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich (USA) and CFW Laboratories (Germany), respectively. Crocin I and crocin II were purchased from Meilunbio (China). All the chemical reagents used here were of analytical grade.</p><p>Samples were analyzed using a Thermo Ultimate 3000 system equipped with an Acquity UPLC® BEH C18 column (1.7 μm, 100 × 2.1 mm). A gradient elution procedure was applied, using the mobile phases acetonitrile containing 0.1% formic acid (A) and water containing 0.1% formic acid (B). The following gradient elution program was used at a flow rate 0.3 mL/min: 0–5 min, 10% A linearly increased to 50% A; 5–8 min, 50% A linearly increased to 90% A; 8–10 min, 90% A linearly increased to 100% A and sustained for 20 min; and 30–31 min, back to 10% A.</p><p>Qualitative analysis of each compound was carried out using liquid chromatography-mass spectrometry (Agilent Technologies 1290 Infinity II LC System and 6545 Q-TOF, with Dual Agilent Jet Stream Electrospray Ionization sources). The drying gas was set at 325 °C with a flow rate of 6 L/min, and the sheath gas was set at 350 °C, with a flow rate of 12.0 L/min. The nebulizer was set at 45 psig, and the VCap was set at 4000 V. The data were analyzed using MassHunter (version B.07.00). The detailed information of mentioned compounds was listed in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00795-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S17, S18.</p><p>Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) experiments were performed on Bruker AV III 600 NMR spectrometer (600 MHz for <sup>1</sup>H NMR and 150 MHz for <sup>13</sup>C NMR) in CDCl<sub>3</sub> (Sigma-Aldrich, USA), and the chemical shifts were given in <i>δ</i> (ppm) with TMS as the internal standard.</p></div></div></section>
